<div id="review_85060646" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/85060646" />
    <a title="El" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/83144-el"><img alt="El" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1479156984p2/83144.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/85060646?book_show_action=true">Jan 14, 2010</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="El" class="user" itemprop="url" name="El" href="/user/show/83144-el">El</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9781573221566" href="/book/show/849180.Affinity">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/83144-el?shelf=20th-centurylit-late">20th-centurylit-late</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer85060646" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12042722431317373626">This second book by Waters takes place again in 19-century England much like her first book, <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/56373.Tipping_the_Velvet' title='Tipping the Velvet by Sarah Waters' rel='nofollow noopener'>Tipping the Velvet</a>. This story takes place not on the stage and in the bedrooms of ladies, but primarily in a women's prison. Margaret is a "Lady Visitor" as part of her rehab after attempting suicide after her father's death. While there she becomes captivated by Selina Dawes, a woman who was imprisoned for her work in spirituality after a seance went awry. Margaret begins researching Selina and fills h</span>
  <span id="freeText12042722431317373626" style="display:none">This second book by Waters takes place again in 19-century England much like her first book, <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/56373.Tipping_the_Velvet" title="Tipping the Velvet by Sarah Waters" rel="nofollow noopener">Tipping the Velvet</a>. This story takes place not on the stage and in the bedrooms of ladies, but primarily in a women's prison. Margaret is a "Lady Visitor" as part of her rehab after attempting suicide after her father's death. While there she becomes captivated by Selina Dawes, a woman who was imprisoned for her work in spirituality after a seance went awry. Margaret begins researching Selina and fills her journal with her findings.<br /><br />Unfortunately this story was not as engrossing as <i>Tipping the Velvet</i>. I read once that Waters had difficulty writing this story, and did not particularly enjoy the process herself. The scenery was portrayed fantastically, which is something I appreciated in her first book as well. But what this book lacks that the first was so good at was creating vivid, sympathetic characters. At no time did I really feel like I <i>knew</i> Margaret or Selina which is unfortunate as they both had a lot of potential. Still, I'm not disappointed exactly - even my favorite authors have had a few cow patties under their belt.</span>
  <a data-text-id="12042722431317373626" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_228810884" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/228810884" />
    <a title="Blair" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/272909-blair"><img alt="Blair" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1493499323p2/272909.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/228810884?book_show_action=true">Oct 29, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Blair" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Blair" href="/user/show/272909-blair">Blair</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Kindle Edition " href="/book/show/11467704-affinity">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/272909-blair?shelf=historical">historical</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/272909-blair?shelf=1990s-release">1990s-release</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/272909-blair?shelf=read-on-kindle">read-on-kindle</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/272909-blair?shelf=ghosts-and-horror">ghosts-and-horror</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer228810884" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16967856928625013672">Set in the mid-1870s, <i>Affinity</i> is the story of lonely Margaret Prior. Nearing thirty, unmarried, and recovering from a series of difficult and upsetting events including the death of her beloved father, she takes up the duties of a 'lady visitor' at London's Millbank prison. Assigned to visit, speak with and offer companionship to the female prisoners, she finds herself developing a particular affection for one inmate - Selina Dawes, an alleged medium imprisoned for fraud and assault. At first, </span>
  <span id="freeText16967856928625013672" style="display:none">Set in the mid-1870s, <i>Affinity</i> is the story of lonely Margaret Prior. Nearing thirty, unmarried, and recovering from a series of difficult and upsetting events including the death of her beloved father, she takes up the duties of a 'lady visitor' at London's Millbank prison. Assigned to visit, speak with and offer companionship to the female prisoners, she finds herself developing a particular affection for one inmate - Selina Dawes, an alleged medium imprisoned for fraud and assault. At first, Margaret's visits are focused on exploring the unfamiliar environment of the prison and meeting the women incarcerated there, as a distraction from her dull and unhappy home life. However, as her friendship with Selina blossoms and she begins to feel increasingly alienated elsewhere, Millbank becomes the centre of Margaret's world, a growing obsession. Her diary makes up the majority of the narrative, intercut with extracts from Selina's earlier journal recounting the events that led to her imprisonment.<br /><br />I have read two of Waters' other novels: <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/45162.Fingersmith" title="Fingersmith by Sarah Waters" rel="nofollow noopener">Fingersmith</a> and <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/6065182.The_Little_Stranger" title="The Little Stranger by Sarah Waters" rel="nofollow noopener">The Little Stranger</a>. The latter is one of the best books I have ever read, and it was because of this that I felt compelled to try <i>Affinity</i>. The two have themes in common, with inexplicable paranormal events and the idea of spirit activity being central to the plot of both. However, <i>Affinity</i> has much more in common with <i>Fingersmith</i>: two female protagonists who take turns narrating the story, and a parade of unpleasant minor characters; a burgeoning lesbian romance, full of suppressed emotion and dull erotic charge; a heavy atmosphere of doom and dread; a series of dreary, depressing settings, from Millbank's cells to the foggy streets of London and Margaret's stifling home life with her unsympathetic mother. Like the heroines of <i>Fingersmith</i>, Margaret and Selina are both thoroughly trapped - Selina, obviously, by her imprisonment, and Margaret by the expectations of society and her family, as well as the illness she cannot name (depression). Treated with sedatives and opiates, which she becomes increasingly dependent upon as the story progresses, Margaret descends into a private, obsessive madness in which the mysteries of Selina's spirit world become very real to her. Selina, meanwhile, remains a more enigmatic character, with her journal conspicuously lacking in emotion.<br /><br />Like <i>The Little Stranger</i>, <i>Affinity</i> has a revelatory ending, with a twist that casts everything that has come before in a different light. However, I have to say I did see this coming: although I didn't guess specifically what the twist would be, I felt <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[there HAD to be something more to Selina's story, as the details of her paranormal experiences seemed so sketchy and unlikely. I found her account of 'Peter Quick' and his appearances so unbelievable that I knew there had to be something more going on beneath the surface of Selina's terse journal entries - also, I just didn't believe that the 'gifts' Selina sent Margaret were in any way magical so the obvious explanation was that there was another link between Selina and the outside world <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span>. Maybe I was primed to anticipate a dramatic, unexpected development because of all the twists in the other Waters books I've read, but I wasn't really all that shocked when it came. I must admit that I didn't think <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[Margaret would be deprived of a happy ending, which just made the whole gloomy, downtrodden story seem even more depressing <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span>! The problem is that if you have your doubts about certain elements of the plot, it's fairly easy to work out the nature, if not the exact details, of the twist.<br /><br />Strangely, this is the third book I have read with a medium (real or fake) as a central character, written by a female author of literary fiction, that I've found drab, dispiriting and bleak. (The others were Hilary Mantel's <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/101923.Beyond_Black" title="Beyond Black by Hilary Mantel" rel="nofollow noopener">Beyond Black</a> and, more recently, <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/11076137.The_Blue_Book" title="The Blue Book by A.L. Kennedy" rel="nofollow noopener">The Blue Book</a> by A.L. Kennedy.) Perhaps a sign (how ironic) that this subject matter should be left alone in future? I do think Waters is a brilliant writer, and I can't fault her wonderfully authentic recreation of a Victorian narrative here. However, as much as the story was compelling and held my interest, it didn't come close to matching the page-turning intensity of <i>Fingersmith</i>, or the brilliant creeping tension of <i>The Little Stranger</i>. While it's worth reading, I wouldn't recommend this as a starting point for newcomers to the author's work.</span>
  <a data-text-id="16967856928625013672" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

              <span id="reviewTextContainer256680861" class="readable"
                    style="display:none"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14872044028487513827">This is the second Sarah Waters book I have read and I find her a most intriguing author. Whilst the first book <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/6065182.The_Little_Stranger' title='The Little Stranger by Sarah Waters' rel='nofollow noopener'>The Little Stranger</a> dealt with the effects of a (?haunted) house on its inhabitants, this book explores spiritualism. Those descriptions don't do the books justice however, they are much more complex and multilayered than I have made them sound.<br/><br/><a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/72929.Affinity' title='Affinity by Sarah Waters' rel='nofollow noopener'>Affinity</a> follows the story of Margaret Prior, a 'lady visitor' to a Victorian women's prison and her growing friendship / relationship with an </span>
  <span id="freeText14872044028487513827" style="display:none">This is the second Sarah Waters book I have read and I find her a most intriguing author. Whilst the first book <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/6065182.The_Little_Stranger" title="The Little Stranger by Sarah Waters" rel="nofollow noopener">The Little Stranger</a> dealt with the effects of a (?haunted) house on its inhabitants, this book explores spiritualism. Those descriptions don't do the books justice however, they are much more complex and multilayered than I have made them sound.<br /><br /><a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/72929.Affinity" title="Affinity by Sarah Waters" rel="nofollow noopener">Affinity</a> follows the story of Margaret Prior, a 'lady visitor' to a Victorian women's prison and her growing friendship / relationship with an inmate, Selina Dawes, accused of violence and spiritualism. The author gradually draws the reader into the other layers in this story, and we see the resemblance between the two women's lives, the incarceration of both, one in prison and the other in her family. The doubts cast by society over the validity of spiritualism and the doubts in Margaret's family of her sanity. <br /><br />The question of the authenticity of spiritualism is addressed - can Selina Dawes really transport items from prison to the home? The methods employed by spiritualists are 'exposed' and hinted at, giving the reader pause to consider whether Selina Dawes has any skills at all or is she a charlatan preying on the vulnerable? How much of what is occuring is due to the mental state of Margaret Prior - is she imagining things? Her fragile mental state requiring frequent heavy doses of medication, and previous suicide attempt, point the reader to question much about her thinking. And yet all the while it is quite believable that there is something other-worldly occuring.<br /><br />The developing affection is well described, never becoming taudry, but is rather shown to be a natural progression, especially for Margaret, whose previous lover is now married to her brother (awkward).<br /><br />Sarah Waters draws the reader in to the atmosphere of the prison to such an extent that I have a clear image in my mind of its sights, sounds and smells, and I can clearly 'see' the prison guards too.<br /><br />This book is highly recommended for those who like to think whilst they read.</span>
  <a data-text-id="14872044028487513827" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer1201221385213738442">When I first finished this book, I rated it 4 stars because my head was still spinning from the very clever twists at the end. But now I come to review the book and it is very definitely a 5 star read for me.<br/><br/>This darkly gothic tale takes part in Victorian London and Waters' writing really paints a vivid and stark portrait of what it must have been like living there, and in particular what life was like in a women's prison. <br/><br/>When I started reading the first chapter, I thought I wasn't going to lik</span>
  <span id="freeText1201221385213738442" style="display:none">When I first finished this book, I rated it 4 stars because my head was still spinning from the very clever twists at the end. But now I come to review the book and it is very definitely a 5 star read for me.<br /><br />This darkly gothic tale takes part in Victorian London and Waters' writing really paints a vivid and stark portrait of what it must have been like living there, and in particular what life was like in a women's prison. <br /><br />When I started reading the first chapter, I thought I wasn't going to like the book. The first chapter was written from the perspective of Selina, the spiritualist who had found herself locked up in prison after one person died and another was injured during a seance. Strangely, in this chapter, the &amp; sign was used instead of the word "and" and for some reason it really disrupted my reading of the story. But then with chapter two, the writing style and POV was switched to that of Margaret, a Lady Visitor who attends the prison which Selina is incarcerated. Once the writing style changed, I was hooked (about 10 pages in). Selina has future chapters in the book and these are in that same style as the first chapter which frustrated me but as the book went on, I no longer noticed it as I was just swept up in the story.<br /><br />Superb writing, building of suspense, setting the scene and ability to transport the reader in to that prison! Some truly creepy moments too and a blinding ending! I thoroughly enjoyed the book.<br /><br />This is my first book by Waters and I am off to start <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/45162.Fingersmith" title="Fingersmith by Sarah Waters" rel="nofollow noopener">Fingersmith</a> now. I have added all her other books to my wishlist.<br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="1201221385213738442" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer13994819006595378717">I've felt many different ways about Sarah Waters novels. The first I read - <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/56373.Tipping_the_Velvet' title='Tipping the Velvet by Sarah Waters' rel='nofollow noopener'>Tipping the Velvet</a> - I loved. I got to the end and turned immediately back to the begining. <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/45162.Fingersmith' title='Fingersmith by Sarah Waters' rel='nofollow noopener'>Fingersmith</a> I really enjoyed, <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/550720.The_Night_Watch' title='The Night Watch by Sarah Waters' rel='nofollow noopener'>The Night Watch</a> I thought was ok and <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/6065182.The_Little_Stranger' title='The Little Stranger by Sarah Waters' rel='nofollow noopener'>The Little Stranger</a> I hated. <br/><br/>So who knew where I was to stand with Affinity?   <span id="freeText13994819006595378717" style="display:none">I've felt many different ways about Sarah Waters novels. The first I read - <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/56373.Tipping_the_Velvet" title="Tipping the Velvet by Sarah Waters" rel="nofollow noopener">Tipping the Velvet</a> - I loved. I got to the end and turned immediately back to the begining. <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/45162.Fingersmith" title="Fingersmith by Sarah Waters" rel="nofollow noopener">Fingersmith</a> I really enjoyed, <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/550720.The_Night_Watch" title="The Night Watch by Sarah Waters" rel="nofollow noopener">The Night Watch</a> I thought was ok and <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/6065182.The_Little_Stranger" title="The Little Stranger by Sarah Waters" rel="nofollow noopener">The Little Stranger</a> I hated. <br /><br />So who knew where I was to stand with Affinity? After all, my feelings on SW run the entire gauntlet. But I was excited when I realised I'd forgotten about this novel of hers and - happily! - I wasn't disappointed.<br /><br />I didn't love it as much as Tipping the Velvet but I still really enjoyed this. Waters is a demon for a twist and although I had half worked it out - the gist but not all the details - I still found it enjoyable. <br /><br />The thing about Affinity is that, for me at least, part of the problem is that you (me) WANT it to be true. This story of two woman - one a prison 'lady visitor' and the other a prisoner - who fall in love and hatch a psychic escape plan is both touching and ridiculous. But, God. Did I want it to be true! <br /><br />And thst's Waters' talent, I think. I wanted no-one to be tricked - well, except me. I do so love a twist.</span>

<span id="freeTextContainer11445049851956408812">It was my first book by Sarah Waters (how come?!) and not the last one, for sure. From the very beginning I got transferred into XIX London and I stayed there for the two days in which I read it (and for me it was quick as a lightening!) I believed in everything, in the characters, in the story, and was experiencing it with the characters. I got invested in the story so much that in the middle I “guessed” the ending – but kept my fingers crossed for myself to be mistaken. <a class='jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction'>(view spoiler)</a><span class='spoilerContainer' style='display: none'>[That was</span></span>
  <span id="freeText11445049851956408812" style="display:none">It was my first book by Sarah Waters (how come?!) and not the last one, for sure. From the very beginning I got transferred into XIX London and I stayed there for the two days in which I read it (and for me it was quick as a lightening!) I believed in everything, in the characters, in the story, and was experiencing it with the characters. I got invested in the story so much that in the middle I “guessed” the ending – but kept my fingers crossed for myself to be mistaken. <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[That was why in the beginning I gave this book 3 stars and not 4.5 it deserves in my opinion – I was so sad and enraged. Not even because of Selina’s choices that made sense and were logical. But because Margaret ended as she feared the most – ridiculed old lady, “not worthy” of love, used and left without means of livelihood that promised her at least some bit of independence. It hurt me so much I was crying when I ended the book. I wanted all London to burn because of that humiliated, heart-broken “spinster”. I’m still bitter when I think about it but after second thought, I believe it is all meant to be that way and it’s the strength of the book, not the weakness. <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span> The atmosphere of mystery, love in the slowest-burn possible and the depression together with the sense of claustrophobia that Margaret experiences are amazing and send shivers down your spine. It also made some distinct point about female oppression, sexuality and independence that it can’t be ignored. It worked well for me and although I’m not going to read it again, I will surely read other Waters’s books.</span>

It wasn't really a surprise for me, since I saw it coming, but the ending just made it a better story. <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[I realized Selina was a liar, and I suspected very early Vigers was Ruth. I thought Mrs Jelf was part of the plan, not another victim of it. I am a skeptic, so I asked myself many times if I believed in the spirits in the story, and I could have buy the flowers and things that they supposedly brought, but the whole Peter Quick thing was too bizarre and I have the feeling Sarah Waters was a better writer than that.  <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span><br /><br />Update 02/04/2020: Now that I am reading my first book by <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/2001717.Daphne_du_Maurier" title="Daphne du Maurier" rel="nofollow noopener">Daphne du Maurier</a>, I can see how similar <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/25334.Sarah_Waters" title="Sarah Waters" rel="nofollow noopener">Sarah Waters</a>'s writing style is to hers.</span>
  <a data-text-id="7873354118620497794" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

<div id="review_3561180519" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3561180519" />
    <a title="Veronique" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/12371602-veronique"><img alt="Veronique" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1590509341p2/12371602.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3561180519?book_show_action=true">Oct 21, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Veronique" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Veronique" href="/user/show/12371602-veronique">Veronique</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback " href="/book/show/18719763-affinity">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/12371602-veronique?shelf=r2020">r2020</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/12371602-veronique?shelf=stars-4-0">stars-4-0</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/12371602-veronique?shelf=historical-fiction">historical-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/12371602-veronique?shelf=lgbt">lgbt</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/12371602-veronique?shelf=mystery">mystery</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/12371602-veronique?shelf=literary">literary</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3561180519" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5850541318379007038">I’d been meaning to read more of Sarah Waters for years and yet hadn’t since loving <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/8913370.Fingersmith' title='Fingersmith by Sarah Waters' rel='nofollow noopener'>Fingersmith</a> far too long ago. Thankfully, Miranda reminded me of it with her October Book Club (<a target='_blank' rel='noopener nofollow' href='https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=f68ktSTdS-E'>https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=f68kt...</a>). <br/><br/>As expected, this is a very well written novel, pulling you into the London of the 1870s, washed out of colour and yet oppressing your senses. The story follows Margaret Prior, a young lady who tries to find purpose by doing charity work, visiting female inmates at the Millban</span>
  <span id="freeText5850541318379007038" style="display:none">I’d been meaning to read more of Sarah Waters for years and yet hadn’t since loving <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/8913370.Fingersmith" title="Fingersmith by Sarah Waters" rel="nofollow noopener">Fingersmith</a> far too long ago. Thankfully, Miranda reminded me of it with her October Book Club (<a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=f68ktSTdS-E">https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=f68kt...</a>). <br /><br />As expected, this is a very well written novel, pulling you into the London of the 1870s, washed out of colour and yet oppressing your senses. The story follows Margaret Prior, a young lady who tries to find purpose by doing charity work, visiting female inmates at the Millbank prison. There she meets the enigmatic, spiritualist, Selina Dawes...<br /><br />The narration starts ever so slowly, divided into the written accounts of both women. Waters takes her time to place all her pieces, precisely, before setting them into action, and then the pace picks up noticeably. Following Margaret, you can’t help but making the comparison between the horrible imprisonment in the jail, with all its sordidness, with the more subtle and yet still restrictive life of women seemingly ‘free’. Voicelessness is closely linked to this too. Actually, the prison functioned like a lens that augmented everything that was happening ‘outside’.<br /><br />Did I get the clue at the beginning? Yes, but that’s probably because I’ve read lots of mysteries and loved watching the TV series The Mentalist. Still, it was fascinating to see how the author orchestrated the whole thing.</span>
  <a data-text-id="5850541318379007038" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('PWUfa4qv31AZi5bWJiuqLTg9XDpsewKmuRdVToE+3Tbypzn2ojG8+UQa8na/bmq2aAKY7jm9tXrpb5HrybwehQ==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('5bKEte/2QIKhxFMr2ObzxqDPs1EnXSsF6SRbhKcAsDwqcKIox2gjK/xVN4tBozNd8PB3hXKbnNm5XJ8h74Jzjw==')})
  }

A paragon of remorseless evil, Barabas befriends and betrays the Turkish invaders and<\/span>\n  <span id=\"freeText12813157361060315362\" style=\"display:none\">The spirit of Machiavelli presides over The Jew of Malta, in which the title character relentlessly plots to maintain and extend his political influence and wealth. A paragon of remorseless evil, Barabas befriends and betrays the Turkish invaders and native Maltese alike, incites a duel between the suitors for his daughter\'s hand, and takes lethal revenge upon a convent of nuns.Both tragedy and farce, this masterpiece of Elizabethan theater reflects the social and political complexities of its age. Christopher Marlowe\'s dramatic hybrid resonates with racial tension, religious conflict, and political intrigue — all of which abounded in 16th-century England. The playwright, who infused each one of his plays with cynical humor and a dark world view, draws upon stereotypes of Muslim and Christian as well as Jewish characters to cast an ironic perspective on all religious beliefs.The immediate success of The Jew of Malta on the Elizabethan stage is presumed to have influenced Marlowe\'s colleague, William Shakespeare, to draw upon the same source material for The Merchant of Venice. The character of Barabas is the prototype for the well-known Shylock, and this drama of his villainy remains a satirical gem in its own right.<\/span>\n  <a data-text-id=\"12813157361060315362\" Į klestinčią kunigaikščio Boriatinskio sodybą, apsuptą lūžtančio nuo gėrybių sodo. Ir sodyba, ir pats kunigaikščių gyvenimas – kaip šventė: amžina, dosni, džiūgaujanti. Tačiau į šį gyvenimą ateina vėlyvas ir niekieno nelaukiamas vaikas. Dukra, kurios gimimas sugriauna idealiai atrodžiusią šeimą. Tusia nuo gimimo yra kitokia. Patriarchalinėje, tradicijų sukaustytoje visuomenėje, kurioje kiekvienas elgiasi ir gyvena taip, kaip diktuoja luomo taisyklės, ji atrodo, lyg būtų atėjusi iš kito laiko. Ji nuo gimimo daro ką nori, elgiasi kaip neįprastai laisvai, šokiruodama aplinkinius. Ji pati sprendžia, kada jai pradėti kalbėti. Tai epinis, plačiais mostais nutapytas anuometinės Rusijos paveikslas. Didžiulis Boriatinskių dvaras – tarsi rusiškas pasaulio modelis: su pirkliais ir valstiečiais, prietarais ir pažanga, mergomis patarnautojomis, sėja ir derliaus nuėmimu, sodu, kiaulėmis ir arkliais.       <div id="aboutAuthor" class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/author/show/25334.Sarah_Waters">About Sarah Waters</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
          <div class='bookAuthorProfile'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__topContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__photoContainer'>
<a href="/author/show/25334.Sarah_Waters"><div class='bookAuthorProfile__photo' style='background-image: url(https://images.gr-assets.com/authors/1409248454p3/25334.jpg);'></div>
</a></div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__widgetContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__name'>
<a href="/author/show/25334.Sarah_Waters">Sarah Waters
</a></div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__followerCount'>
6,574
followers
</div>

</div>
</div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer9711074513347684784"><i>Sarah Waters</i> is a British novelist. She is best known for her first novel, <i>Tipping the Velvet</i>, as well the novels that followed, including <i>Affinity</i>, <i>Fingersmith</i>, and <i>The Night Watch</i>.<br/><br/>Waters attended university, earning degrees in English literature. Before writing novels Waters worked as an academic, earning a doctorate and teaching. Waters went directly from her doctoral thesis to her first novel.</span>
  <span id="freeText9711074513347684784" style="display:none"><i>Sarah Waters</i> is a British novelist. She is best known for her first novel, <i>Tipping the Velvet</i>, as well the novels that followed, including <i>Affinity</i>, <i>Fingersmith</i>, and <i>The Night Watch</i>.<br /><br />Waters attended university, earning degrees in English literature. Before writing novels Waters worked as an academic, earning a doctorate and teaching. Waters went directly from her doctoral thesis to her first novel. It was during the process of writing her thesis that she thought she would write a novel; she began as soon as the thesis was complete.</span>
  <a data-text-id="9711074513347684784" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

</div>
</div>
